  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1d4e8617-c84d-4d26-b849-bbeb69ab4d93'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer18346980581142843721">‘It is stripped off - the paper - in great patches . . . The colour is repellent . . . In the places where it isn’t faded and where the sun is just so - I can see a strange, provoking, formless sort of figure, that seems to skulk about . . .’<br/><br/>Based on the author’s own experiences, 'The Yellow Wallpaper' is the chilling tale of a woman driven to the brink of insanity by the </span>
  <span id="freeText18346980581142843721" style="display:none">‘It is stripped off - the paper - in great patches . . . The colour is repellent . . . In the places where it isn’t faded and where the sun is just so - I can see a strange, provoking, formless sort of figure, that seems to skulk about . . .’<br /><br />Based on the author’s own experiences, 'The Yellow Wallpaper' is the chilling tale of a woman driven to the brink of insanity by the ‘rest cure’ prescribed after the birth of her child. Isolated in a crumbling colonial mansion, in a room with bars on the windows, the tortuous pattern of the yellow wallpaper winds its way into the recesses of her mind.</span>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/5662008-kei-smith">Kei Smith</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">One of the major points in the story is that he doesn't pay any attention to what <i>she</i> wants or what she would like to do. She says she would like to g<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">One of the major points in the story is that he doesn't pay any attention to what <i>she</i> wants or what she would like to do. She says she would like to go outside -- why not let her go outside? She says she would like to be around people -- why not let her be around people? The "husband knows best" perspective -- even if he <i>is</i> a doctor -- is perhaps what hurts her most.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_1664573012" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1664573012" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1664573012?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8217236-the-yellow-wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1664573012" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12194137283821530740"><b>”If a physician of high standing, and one’s own husband, assures friends and relatives that there is really nothing the matter with one but temporary nervous depression---a slight hysterical tendency---what is one to do?”</b><br/><br/>Well, one must quit being a silly goose and get better. <br/><br/>The baby is fine; thank goodness, the baby is fine. It is safe, safe in another room. Away from the horrid yellow wallpaper. This wallpaper is an artistic monstrosity, an assault on the senses. It is so yellow it reeks of..</span>
  <span id="freeText12194137283821530740" style="display:none"><b>”If a physician of high standing, and one’s own husband, assures friends and relatives that there is really nothing the matter with one but temporary nervous depression---a slight hysterical tendency---what is one to do?”</b><br><br>Well, one must quit being a silly goose and get better. <br><br>The baby is fine; thank goodness, the baby is fine. It is safe, safe in another room. Away from the horrid yellow wallpaper. This wallpaper is an artistic monstrosity, an assault on the senses. It is so yellow it reeks of...yellow. <br><br><i>”It is the strangest yellow, that wall-paper! It makes me think of all the yellow things I ever saw---not beautiful ones like buttercups, but old foul, bad yellow things.”</i><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Yellow20Wallpaper_zpskkl3c7au.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532608387i/26031522.jpg" alt=" photo Yellow20Wallpaper_zpskkl3c7au.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>In such a room, too, a beautiful room with windows and light. John brought her here for a vacation away from the daily cares of their normal lives, to help her with her nervousness. She hasn’t been the same since the baby was born. She knows that, not that she was ever normal before, but something has shifted, an awareness of self that is tuned to a different frequency. <br><br><b>The wallpaper is hideous. It claws at her mind.</b> <br><br>She should be happy, after all there is the baby. The girl is taking such good care of her baby. <i>”I cry at nothing, and cry most of the time. Of course I don’t when John is here, or anybody else, but when I am alone.”</i><br><br><b>It doesn’t happen if no one sees it or hears it.</b> <br><br>In the middle of the night, lit by moonlight, the wallpaper moves. She touches it; she studies it. <i>”The faint figure behind seemed to shake the pattern, just as if she wanted to get out.”</i><br><br>She must write. They don’t want her to write, but those who write must write. It is not enough to write in the mind. The words must be taken from her mind to make room for more words. Words are precision, clarity, sanity. <br><br>John thinks it is best for her to rest in the room. The room has become the extent of her universe, and the wallpaper is more alive than she is. <i>”All those strangled heads and bulbous eyes and waddling fungus growths just shriek with derision!”</i> <br><br>If she escapes the wallpaper, what then? <br><br>I kept thinking as I was reading this story that I hope Robert Louis Stevenson had the chance to read it, but probably not. This story was published in 1892. He bought a place in Samoa in 1890 and was cut off from most of the literary world until his death in 1894. It is certainly a story that would have appealed to his own obsession with the horrors of the mind. The building tension from a fairly typical domestic scene to the final horrors is so gothic and is still sending a cascade of shivers down my spine. Charlotte Perkins Gilman grew up under the tutelage of women who believed strongly in equality. Her father’s aunts, Isabella Beecher Hooker, a suffragist, Harriet Beecher Stowe, and Catharine Beecher, helped raise her after her father abandoned the family. Charlotte tried marriage. She even had a baby. Those attempts at being “normal” didn’t work out, but it did give us this wonderful autobiographical and, in my opinion, nearly perfect short story. <br><br>Charlotte grew up in an era where it was difficult for women to have any say in their fate. If they became too “hysterical,” they were locked up in an insane asylum. They were seen as delicate creatures, incapable of making rational decisions for themselves. I still believe that many women suffer from postpartum depression and still feel the need to hide their symptoms. After all, aren’t they supposed to be joyfully happy to be new mothers? There are too many women still trapped in the walls behind yellow wallpaper. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Charlotte20Perkins20Gilman_zpsvimqurya.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532608387i/26031523.jpg" alt=" photo Charlotte20Perkins20Gilman_zpsvimqurya.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>When Charlotte Perkins Gilman finds out she is dying of breast cancer in 1935, she takes control of her fate. In her suicide note she writes that she <b>”chose chloroform over cancer”</b>. In the end, she escapes the yellow wallpaper on her own terms. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12194137283821530740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" href="/user/show/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies">Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108224585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13496454899109711899"><blockquote>
  <i>If a physician of high standing, and one's own husband, assures friends and relatives that there is really nothing the matter with one but temporary nervous depression—a slight hysterical tendency—what is one to do? </i>
</blockquote>This may not be a ghost story, but it is a tale of horror just the same. The most frightening books do not make me cower underneath my covers in the dark. They give the feeling of despair, they make the reader empathize with the darkness and emotional turmoil of the narrator. The</span>
  <span id="freeText13496454899109711899" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>If a physician of high standing, and one's own husband, assures friends and relatives that there is really nothing the matter with one but temporary nervous depression—a slight hysterical tendency—what is one to do? </i>
</blockquote>This may not be a ghost story, but it is a tale of horror just the same. The most frightening books do not make me cower underneath my covers in the dark. They give the feeling of despair, they make the reader empathize with the darkness and emotional turmoil of the narrator. They make one feel claustrophobic.<br /><br />Women's mental problems have always been dismissed as hysteria, from the beginning of time. It is this overwhelming diminution of mental problems that led to so many being institutionalized in the past, and it is the reason why the repressed Victorian woman was such a tremendous symbol of the age.<br /><br />That mentally unsound character recurs again in this little short story, an exceptionally fine example of how a woman, neglected, belittled, dismissed, descends into insanity.</span>

<div id="review_1955760802" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1955760802" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1955760802?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1955760802" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7140382682590365919">The Yellow Wall Paper by Charlotte Perkins Gilman written in 1892 is considered a story that is a leading feminist view about a woman's place in a traditional marriage during that time period. Gilman herself was an intellectual voice and staunch supporter of women's rights in marriage. Most leading magazines refused to publish this story and it was lost for many years. Once recovered, it has become an often talked about story in many literary anthologies. <br/><br/>The protagonist of this story is taken t</span>
  <span id="freeText7140382682590365919" style="display:none">The Yellow Wall Paper by Charlotte Perkins Gilman written in 1892 is considered a story that is a leading feminist view about a woman's place in a traditional marriage during that time period. Gilman herself was an intellectual voice and staunch supporter of women's rights in marriage. Most leading magazines refused to publish this story and it was lost for many years. Once recovered, it has become an often talked about story in many literary anthologies. <br /><br />The protagonist of this story is taken to the country to recover from an unnamed illness. Upon finding out that the nurse cares for the baby, one is lead to believe that the illness is postpartum depression, leading to a mental breakdown. The protagonist's husband and brother are both doctors and believe that the country would help lead to her recovery. Both men believe that women are for the most part subservient to men. The husband calls her my girl and lamb, all tender words of affection, denouncing her feelings. As a result, the suffering woman is brought to the country against her better wishes. <br /><br />While in the country, she stays in a room with crumbling, yellow wallpaper. Rather than improving her health, the patterns lead her to a greater state of mental illness. She creates stories out of the supposed paisley design and has delusions that a woman from the paper is out to get her. Before it became decent to speak of mental illness, the best way to gain one's health was through treat and fresh air. In this case, the rest caused the protagonist to suffer greatly, leading to her demise, much like the story's author. <br /><br />The Yellow Wall Paper was not as much of a literary masterpiece but a landmark noting a woman's place in a marriage and what could occur to women following the birth of her children. Gilman's work is now widely studied but denounced upon publication. More than one hundred years later, The Yellow Wall Paper is a worthy study, and a story that leads to invoking discussions. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7140382682590365919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3209809362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Merphy Napier" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41973734-merphy-napier"><img alt="Merphy Napier" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524921716p2/41973734.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1934467065?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1934467065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14390789046221985442"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488975940i/22173611.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>"<i>My wallpaper and I are fighting a duel to the death. One or the other of us has to go.</i>"<br/>Oscar Wilde’s alleged final words. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.internationalwomensday.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>International Women’s Day</a> is perfect for reviewing this chilling short story, written by a utopian feminist in 1890. (Yes, I opened with Wilde, but I couldn’t resist, and he was also a victim of sexually-related prejudice.)<br/> <br/><b>The Story</b> <br/><br/>John’s wife. Jennie’s sister-in-law. A baby’s mother. She is anonymous. She writes furtively. <br/><br/>She is physically and mentally weak from “tempora</span>
  <span id="freeText14390789046221985442" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488975940i/22173611.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>"<i>My wallpaper and I are fighting a duel to the death. One or the other of us has to go.</i>"<br>Oscar Wilde’s alleged final words. <br><br><a href="https://www.internationalwomensday.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">International Women’s Day</a> is perfect for reviewing this chilling short story, written by a utopian feminist in 1890. (Yes, I opened with Wilde, but I couldn’t resist, and he was also a victim of sexually-related prejudice.)<br> <br><b>The Story</b> <br><br>John’s wife. Jennie’s sister-in-law. A baby’s mother. She is anonymous. She writes furtively. <br><br>She is physically and mentally weak from “temporary nervous depression - a slight hysterical tendency”. She is confined to rest in an attic room of a rented house, on the advice of her doctor husband. A room with such ghastly yellow wallpaper that it becomes an obsession - exacerbating, rather than alleviating her mental instability. <br><br>But they’re staying in a hyperbolically “beautiful place” with a “delicious garden”. Everyone is kind and caring and lovely. Her doting, suffocating husband. Her sister-in-law running the house, helping care for her. And Mary, who “is so good with the baby”. It’s clear to the reader that the infantalising constraints and prohibition of mental and physical stimuli are as life-sapping as any leaden cage, despite the shiny padlock. <br><br>She is cast deeper into the pit of helplessness - her husband addresses her as “little girl”. What’s less certain is the motive of her carers. Is she inherently mad, being manipulated, or just overly-pampered?<br><br>When she chastises herself, you hear the insidious and undermining words of others: “I get unreasonably angry with John sometimes. I'm sure I never used to be so sensitive. I think it is due to this nervous condition.” <br><br>The question is how to get better: compliance, defiance, or psychological escape. <br><br><b>The Wall-Paper</b><br><br>The descriptions start off comically horrid, but realistic:<br><br>“<i>One of those sprawling flamboyant patterns committing every artistic sin. It is dull enough to confuse the eye in following, pronounced enough to constantly irritate and provoke study, and when you follow the lame uncertain curves for a little distance they suddenly commit suicide - plunge off at outrageous angles, destroy themselves in unheard of contradictions… a smouldering unclean yellow.</i>”<br><br>As the narrator loses her hold on reality, the patterns seem to morph in sinister but enticing ways, with the changing light. I was reminded of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magic_Eye" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Magic Eye</b>
</a> pictures that were all the rage for a few years in the 1990s, but which I never learned to “see” in 3D. <br><br><b>The True Story</b><br><br>This is semi-autobiographical, but it wasn’t post-partum psychosis that ended Charlotte Perkins Gilman’s life, nor the frustrations of patriarchal and controlling rest cures. It was not even the breast cancer that tried its damndest. In her own words, she took control and "chose chloroform over cancer". <br><br><b>Nowadays</b><br><br>With an autocratic misogynist recently elected as the most powerful person in the world, the suppression of women’s autonomy, whether clothed in good intentions or not, is as important an issue as ever. The war for equality is not yet won. <br><br><b>Sources, Notes, and Links</b><br><br>You can read the story, free, on Project Gutenberg, <a href="https://www.gutenberg.org/files/1952/1952-h/1952-h.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.<br><br>For a slightly different, but equally provoking take on a similar situation, see <b>The Victorian Chaise Longue</b> (my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83707194" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), in which an invalid young mother in 1940s/50s is transported (whether for real, or in her delusions) to the mind and body of a woman with a similar condition, but in earlier, even less enlightened times.<br><br>Image source for yellow wallpaper:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://static.enotes.com/images/covers%2Fyellow-wallpaper.jpg">https://static.enotes.com/images/cove...</a><br><br>The compulsion to pick and peel one’s skin is called <b>dermatillomania or neurotic excoriation</b>. It is usually considered on the OCD spectrum: a repetitive, ritualistic, and tension reducing form of self-harm. Peeling wall-paper is clearly less harmful to the body, as to the state of mind…?</span>
  <a data-text-id="14390789046221985442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2094987737" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2094987737" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2094987737?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2017-completed">xx2017-completed</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2094987737" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10520658857819771887">This is not a happy story – not even in the slightest. Our protagonist and her husband and sister-in-law are spending 3 months in a rented home during renovations of their own home. The woman recently had a baby and has not been able to recover her energy nor the will to accomplish anything. She is a writer but her husband, a physician, tells her not to write because it will only add to her fanciful state of being.<br/><br/>On the one hand, he is very controlling – and his wife sees that as a display of l</span>
  <span id="freeText10520658857819771887" style="display:none">This is not a happy story – not even in the slightest. Our protagonist and her husband and sister-in-law are spending 3 months in a rented home during renovations of their own home. The woman recently had a baby and has not been able to recover her energy nor the will to accomplish anything. She is a writer but her husband, a physician, tells her not to write because it will only add to her fanciful state of being.<br /><br />On the one hand, he is very controlling – and his wife sees that as a display of love. On the other hand, he carries her up the stairs to conserve her energy and tells her to get well quickly because he can’t live without her – and she is unable to respond appropriately.<br /><br />Their bedroom has yellow wallpaper which she becomes fixated on. She describes the pattern and the ‘sick yellow’ of its colour many times, and you can watch her mind grappling with reality the more she talks about the wallpaper. It becomes an obsession, and the more she sees, the more we can see that she is on a slippery slope with no-one to pull her back.<br /><br />This sad story of a psychological breakdown spirals from low energy and spirits into a very dark place in its few pages. It serves as a cautionary tale because when asked, she insisted she was fine except for being tired. She hid her feelings from her husband and sister-in-law, and what was at first a desire to “put a good face on it” became dangerous deception and deliberate avoidance. Even toward herself.<br /><br />This is an amazing piece of writing and worth reading for the experience of better understanding mental illness and how it can subtly infect all areas of a person’s life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10520658857819771887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4143987524778061540">The Yellow Wallpaper is a short but powerful masterpiece in which Charlotte Perkins Gilman offers insight into oppression and madness. It remains (despite being written in 1892) as relevant as it is haunting. Many people know the story of how Gilman's narrator is forbidden to write by her husband/doctor and fights for autonomy in the patterns of wallpaper. Liberation from his and society's oppression of women is only available in this internal struggle which ultimately leads to a mental breakdow</span>
  <span id="freeText4143987524778061540" style="display:none">The Yellow Wallpaper is a short but powerful masterpiece in which Charlotte Perkins Gilman offers insight into oppression and madness. It remains (despite being written in 1892) as relevant as it is haunting. Many people know the story of how Gilman's narrator is forbidden to write by her husband/doctor and fights for autonomy in the patterns of wallpaper. Liberation from his and society's oppression of women is only available in this internal struggle which ultimately leads to a mental breakdown and loss of identity. </span>

<div id="review_1260801194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260801194" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1260801194?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8217236-the-yellow-wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=gutenberg-freebie">gutenberg-freebie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1260801194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4449233959566269098">The first time I read this 1892 short story, years ago, in a collection of horror stories, I thought awful and very creepy things were really happening to the main character; i.e., weird fungus-growing wallpaper and a weirder lady actually hiding in the wallpaper pattern of a young wife's room in their vacation home. <----- I was a little young and often oblivious to subtext. <br/><br/>On second read (or probably first read for most people ...), it's clear that the horror is of a different sort: the main </span>
  <span id="freeText4449233959566269098" style="display:none">The first time I read this 1892 short story, years ago, in a collection of horror stories, I thought awful and very creepy things were really happening to the main character; i.e., weird fungus-growing wallpaper and a weirder lady actually hiding in the wallpaper pattern of a young wife's room in their vacation home. &lt;----- I was a little young and often oblivious to subtext. <br><br>On second read (or probably first read for most people ...), it's clear that the horror is of a different sort: the main character, a young wife suffering from anxiety or depression, has been isolated and kept inactive "for her own good," and she is slowly going psychotic. It's still quite creepy, but in a very different way.<br><br>There's a distinct layer of early feminism in this story, as well as a strong implication that the main character might have been able to work through her mental problems if she'd been allowed to do something interesting and productive rather than being pressured and forced into idleness. Apparently this kind of enforced rest and confinement was a standard medical treatment at the time, especially for women, who were deemed the more fragile sex. The author, Charlotte Gillman, felt strongly that this kind of treatment was counter-productive to mental health, rather than a cure.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429657890i/14618327._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="185" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>This makes a nice companion read to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/899492.The_Tell_Tale_Heart" title="The Tell-Tale Heart by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tell-Tale Heart</a>, another classic but very different story of mental illness.<br><br>Gutenberg freebie <a href="https://www.gutenberg.org/files/1952/1952-h/1952-h.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4449233959566269098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1907689863" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1907689863" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1907689863?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8217236-the-yellow-wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1907689863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7393446967297085199">“He says that with my imaginative power and habit of story-making, a nervous weakness like mine is sure to lead to all manner of excited fancies, and that I ought to use my will and good sense to check the tendency. So I try.”<br/><br/>Read in conjunction with Ibsen’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37793.A_Doll_s_House' title='A Doll's House by Henrik Ibsen' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Doll's House</a>, this short story takes a darker turn than the play, refusing to offer a way out of a dilemma in 19th century traditional society.<br/><br/>The story of a young married woman with an infant, who is patronised and controlled by her husba</span>
  <span id="freeText7393446967297085199" style="display:none">“He says that with my imaginative power and habit of story-making, a nervous weakness like mine is sure to lead to all manner of excited fancies, and that I ought to use my will and good sense to check the tendency. So I try.”<br /><br />Read in conjunction with Ibsen’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37793.A_Doll_s_House" title="A Doll's House by Henrik Ibsen" rel="nofollow noopener">A Doll's House</a>, this short story takes a darker turn than the play, refusing to offer a way out of a dilemma in 19th century traditional society.<br /><br />The story of a young married woman with an infant, who is patronised and controlled by her husband to the point of losing her sanity, is creepy, relevant, and not dated at all.<br /><br />Two mindsets and worldviews clash. <br /><br />On one side, there is the “rational husband”, a physician, who calls his wife “little girl” and forces her to passivity, as he claims agitation and stimulation are feeding her imagination in a detrimental way. He keeps her under surveillance, and she is asked to sleep and rest as much as possible, avoiding any kind of activity that can spark independent thoughts.<br /><br />On the other hand, there is the young woman herself, with a strong wish to express herself creatively in writing, opposing the so-called benevolent dictatorship in secret, hiding her true feelings and thoughts in front of the husband, who is “very caring and loving, and hardly lets me stir without special direction”. And he “hates to have me write a word”.<br /><br />The yellow wallpaper in her room becomes an obsessive symbol for the intellectual oppression the young woman experiences. It increasingly chokes her, until she lets go of her resistance and loses her sanity along with her hope to ever be able to live up to the limited version of life her prison guard is willing to grant her. <br /><br />Her description of the yellow wallpaper is a mirror of her internal suffering, the contraction she feels and cannot solve. And it is an ominous sign of the only way she sees out of her hopeless dependency on a man who does not see her as a thinking human being, but rather as a decorative piece of furniture in his possession:<br /><br />“It is dull enough to confuse the eye in following, pronounced enough to constantly irritate and provoke study, and when you follow the lame, uncertain curves for a little distance they suddenly commit suicide - plunge off at outrageous angles, destroy themselves in unheard of contradictions.”<br /><br />The fight she puts up against the symbolical wallpaper makes one remember Oscar Wilde’s alleged last words: “My wallpaper and I are fighting a duel to the death. One or the other of us has to go.”<br /><br />Behind the irony and wit, there is a sense of deadly pain in the face of lost beauty and aesthetic value. Whether or not Oscar Wilde spoke those words, he died just a couple of years after the publication of this short story, a broken man after years in prison which destroyed his spirit and will to create. He too was a victim of a dominant heterosexual, male society, which could accept no exceptions to their preferred way of living: in full control of all aspects of community, especially creative and sexual practices. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18521.A_Room_of_One_s_Own" title="A Room of One's Own by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">A Room of One's Own</a>, so necessary to creative processes according to Virginia Woolf’s idea, turns into a prison if there is no freedom of thought and movement to feed imagination, and no financial independence to be able to make a choice. The room, supervised by "benevolent" authority, turns into a dystopian scenario in the spirit of Orwell. <br /><br />Thoughtcrime and doublethink were well-known to intelligent, captive women long before "1984" named them properly.<br /><br />Still readable, enjoyable, and thought-provoking! Recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7393446967297085199" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_729836800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729836800" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/729836800?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8217236-the-yellow-wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-e-h">read-authors-e-h</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=around-the-world-challenge">around-the-world-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=19th-century-ish">19th-century-ish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=philosophy-sociology-psychology">philosophy-sociology-psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer729836800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14257029291412400782"><b>The Yellow Wallpaper</b> is a short novella from 1892, which has become a classic of the genre. It is a claustrophobic depiction of what would then be described as a woman's descent into madness, but now sounds more like severe post-natal depression. The story consists of passages from a secret journal, kept by the woman, Jane, who is losing her grip on reality. The narrator is confined to the upstairs bedroom of a house by her doctor husband, John, who diagnoses a <i>"temporary nervous depression - a </i></span>
  <span id="freeText14257029291412400782" style="display:none"><b>The Yellow Wallpaper</b> is a short novella from 1892, which has become a classic of the genre. It is a claustrophobic depiction of what would then be described as a woman's descent into madness, but now sounds more like severe post-natal depression. The story consists of passages from a secret journal, kept by the woman, Jane, who is losing her grip on reality. The narrator is confined to the upstairs bedroom of a house by her doctor husband, John, who diagnoses a <i>"temporary nervous depression - a slight hysterical tendency -"</i>. The windows of the room are barred, and there is a gate across the top of the stairs so that she has limited access to the rest of the house. She is also forbidden from working by her husband, whom she claims to comply with because he is a doctor. <br /><br />It is not difficult to see how these constraints would exacerbate any tendency to depression! This story depicts the prevailing attitudes in the 19th century toward women, in particular their physical and mental health, promoting the view that they should live and be defined entirely by domestic considerations. Jane's husband is kindly and insufferably paternalistic, <i>""Bless her little heart!" said he with a big hug, "she shall be as sick as she pleases!""</i> referring to her indulgently as his <i>"little girl"</i>. Charlotte Perkins Gilman was an author, philosopher, socialist and feminist. Her stories both analyse and criticise the role of women in society, at a time when men were very much dominant. The contemporary view is that such women were oppressed by their position in a patriarchal society. <br /><br />In several of her later stories Gilman deals with a male-dominated medical establishment attempting to silence its women patients. In this one the narrator expresses the views that she should work instead of rest, and that she should go out in society more, instead of remaining isolated. She also thinks that she should not be separated and "protected" from her child, but should be able to see her child and allowed to be a mother. This is a modern perspective, and very much ahead of its time. True to the current conventions of behaviour though, Jane is silent, powerless, and passive, accepting her doctor-husband's authority in all things. It was stated by a medical journal of the time, that a physician must <i>"assume a tone of authority"</i> and that the idea of a "cured" woman was one who became <i>"subdued, docile, silent, and above all subject to the will and voice of the physician." </i><br /><br />The writing itself uses sentences with short interjections; questions burst through, as the narrator becomes increasingly delirious. This makes for a very unsettling read. One interpretation could be that since she has been forbidden to read or write, the given medical reason being that her "hysteria" needs "rest", she then starts to "read" the wallpaper, and feels increasingly trapped behind it. She first describes the wallpaper saying, <i>"the colour is repellent, almost revolting; a smouldering unclean yellow, strangely faded by the slow-turning sunlight." </i>It develops into an <i>"optic horror, like a lot of wallowing seaweeds in full chase... interminable grotesques"</i> She yearns for freedom, seeing through her bars to the outside, <i>"A lovely country, too, full of great elms and velvet meadows."</i> <br /><br />She becomes obsessed by the wallpaper, its pattern appearing to change, <i>"all those strangled heads and bulbous eyes and waddling fungus growths just shriek with derision!"</i> The colour becomes more and more loathsome to her with a foul smell emanating from it. At night she is able to see a woman behind bars, trapped within its complicated design.<i> "The woman behind shakes it!"</i> The delusions increase, as does Jane's response to them. The ending is ambiguous, depending on how the reader has interpreted the story. Does she escape? Does she slip into irrevocable psychosis? Does she murder her husband?<br /><br />Clearly though, this story is about disempowering women, even to the point of forbidding the tools for writing, in case "Jane" manages to express her own identity in that way. The bars and trapped woman are originally symbolic of the narrator's own confinement, but eventually she becomes subsumed in the many images of women that she sees. <br /><br /><b>The Yellow Wallpaper</b> originated in Gilman's own experience, when she suffered from depression, and was ordered to lead a similar life to that of the narrator of this story. An eminent specialist prescribed a rest cure, recommending her to live a domestic a life as possible. She was only allowed two hours of mental stimulation a day, and writing materials were banned. She followed this directive for three months, becoming increasingly desperate. Eventually she felt herself slipping into a worse mental state, so rebelled and wrote <b>The Yellow Wallpaper</b> as a sort of therapy for herself, as well as alerting the public to what she considered a seriously misguided form of treatment. She said the story was, <i>"not intended to drive people crazy, but to save people from being driven crazy, and it worked."</i> Sometimes it is viewed simply as a horror story, but it is horrifying to a modern reader in additional ways to merely its gothic feel. <br /><br /><i>"There are things in that wallpaper that nobody knows but me, or ever will... so many of those creeping women, and they creep so fast."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14257029291412400782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23653968" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23653968" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/777369-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1283427299p2/777369.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23653968?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/777369-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/777369-jessica?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/777369-jessica?shelf=innovative-fiction">innovative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/777369-jessica?shelf=books-about-depression">books-about-depression</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23653968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4528204332107448708">This has got to be one of the most impressive short stories ever written, up there with the very best. Written in the late 1800's, it is surprisingly modern in its form & content. When I was an undergraduate, Charlotte Perkins Gilman was an undiscovered writer, but thankfully she's been very much discovered now: I've read her nonfiction ('Women and Economics'--very forward-thinking re: communal kitchens and daycare) and her utopian novel, 'Herland.' She also has some other terrific short stories</span>
  <span id="freeText4528204332107448708" style="display:none">This has got to be one of the most impressive short stories ever written, up there with the very best. Written in the late 1800's, it is surprisingly modern in its form &amp; content. When I was an undergraduate, Charlotte Perkins Gilman was an undiscovered writer, but thankfully she's been very much discovered now: I've read her nonfiction ('Women and Economics'--very forward-thinking re: communal kitchens and daycare) and her utopian novel, 'Herland.' She also has some other terrific short stories, "If I Were a Man," for example and a mystery novel. None is as famous as "The Yellow Wallpaper," however. What's great about this story is that I've found it reprinted in horror anthologies, women's fiction anthologies, college readers, texts on madness...It's a masterful example of an unreliable narrator and a woman's descent into madness. A wife is prescribed bed rest for what appears to be postpartum depression, is confined to a room w/ sickly yellow overly ornate wallpaper...and goes mad from inactivity, lack of meaningful stimulation. Don't want to spoil it by saying any more, if you haven't already read this great short story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4528204332107448708" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1940951891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13690575518198946748">Clever, eerie little story, which I highly recommend to anyone who thinks that depending on a caring spouse is all you need to be happy. Sometimes it's not, and it even can be harmful ; especially if your wallpaper happens to be yellow.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2095904117" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2095904117" />

I found it irritating. <br/><br/>This is actually one of those books I wish I didn't read. <span id="freeTextContainer7649553178919849134">This is an extremely eerie and haunting story of a woman being depressed and not properly helped by her husband and other so called expert in a male dominated society. She begins to see patterns and faces within the wallpaper. The colour yellow is very important here. Does the confinement to that room has any positive impact on her health? Is there a cure for this woman and more important does the story end well? You really should give remarkable story a read. It's definitely worth more than one</span>

<div id="review_642889525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/642889525" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/642889525?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone with a desire to understand how they're trapped by life.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=traditional-fiction">traditional-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer642889525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18436454397991124321">I was reminded of this little piece by a fellow reviewer and while I read it way back in college, several things still stick in my mind.<br/> <br/>First, the prevalent psychology of Freud during the time-period: This novel portrays the kind of circular thinking that could happen to anyone in that particular time and station. Any person of a protected, apparently weak, and especially underclass station could find the confines so stifling that it might break their mind. Of course, this isn't to say that eve</span>
  <span id="freeText18436454397991124321" style="display:none">I was reminded of this little piece by a fellow reviewer and while I read it way back in college, several things still stick in my mind.<br /> <br />First, the prevalent psychology of Freud during the time-period: This novel portrays the kind of circular thinking that could happen to anyone in that particular time and station. Any person of a protected, apparently weak, and especially underclass station could find the confines so stifling that it might break their mind. Of course, this isn't to say that every woman had it this bad, or that they had weak minds. I'm just saying that Freud pointed out something that was happening during this time-period and all of a sudden he gets catapulted into prominence for stating the blinding obvious. Going crazy was an escape.<br /><br />This led to the arrival of hoards of writers, revolutionaries, men and women of all walks of life all deciding that they'd show how much they weren't influenced by Freud. There was the huge push to make things equal between the sexes.<br /><br />Hell, I think that part was very healthy. Ms. Gilman was a perfect revolutionary. She showed us how insane a person could get being put into that society, under those social rules and regulations, and even made her character sympathetic. <br /><br />This is one of those works where it is so much more satisfying to read when we understand where it came from. It's even worse when we understand that this was pretty much a regular part of the times.<br /><br />And then, there's Oscar Wilde.<br />He had a speech on his deathbed (perhaps apocryphal), where he saw the ugly purple wallpaper on the wall next to his deathbed and made a pronouncement, "Either this wallpaper goes, or I go!"<br /><br />And so he died.<br /><br />Death by wallpaper.<br /><br />Was this a commentary? Who knows. Perhaps it was a slight twist and turn in medium, a hidden knife, a big idea slammed by wit.<br /><br />But then, I'm only a man, but I'm proud to say that this story sent me on a long kick of feminism literature back in college. I'm sorry to see that the whole subject is so out of vogue. The backlash backlashed and backlashed again briefly and backlashed until I'm hella unsure where the pendulum has landed. I'm pleased to see it still lives a bit in SF and Fantasy, but but the rest of the genres seem to backsliding more often than they get it right. <br /><br />I mean, what the hell is a Romance novel, except a means to pigeonhole women into a narrowly confined role and teach them to stare at the pretty wallpaper? Some YA novels feature nothing but abusive and truly creep-the-fuck-out characters. Where the hell is the lost ideal of equality between the sexes? All I see these days is frustrated sexual fantasies that rely more on power plays than love.<br /><br />Someone, please let me know where I can get some relief!<br /><br />Anyway, I always liked this story, and it allowed me to flex my imagination and enjoy the surrealism of the literature of the day in a way a little more accessible than others of the type that I just couldn't get into as much. <br /><br />It was still a mindfuq, and put into perspective, I think the novelette gave a great deal of meaning to women. People's perceptions of themselves change over time, obviously, reacting to past mistakes, past preconceptions, but as a cross-gender analysis, I have to say that no one is completely free of the wallpaper.<br /><br />Anyone can be caught up in their social roles. I know I've felt as trapped as our crazy protagonist. It's not just women who have needed to gain a measure of self-awareness.<br /><br /> We all need to say, "Enough is Enough, Already!"</span>
  <a data-text-id="18436454397991124321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4296809489694822098">I typed the title into the search just to see if it would come up. I had no idea that this was a classic work. I never could recall the author's name, but from the reviews, I can see that I am not alone in how it still sits with me decades later. <br/><br/>I was only 13 or 14 years old when I sat in on my aunt's college literature class. I sat in the back, and the teacher gave me a black and white copy of the text so I could read along with the class. I remember the debate raged on in the class, but we re</span>
  <span id="freeText4296809489694822098" style="display:none">I typed the title into the search just to see if it would come up. I had no idea that this was a classic work. I never could recall the author's name, but from the reviews, I can see that I am not alone in how it still sits with me decades later. <br /><br />I was only 13 or 14 years old when I sat in on my aunt's college literature class. I sat in the back, and the teacher gave me a black and white copy of the text so I could read along with the class. I remember the debate raged on in the class, but we read very little there. <br /><br />Later that night, while everyone was asleep, I read the whole story alone in our dark attic apartment. It wasn't that I scared easy or that I was too young for the story, it was just so intense, so real, I guess I thought it was so possible...<br /><br />I looked at everything different from then on. I thought anywhere could be a jail and anyone your jailer. I knew I could see patterns in the sky, in the dark, if you closed and opened your eyes rapidly, in markings on the floor, in the terrible paneling on our walls, but I would never mention this to anyone, least I never am let out again.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2036674022" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2036674022" />
    <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen"><img alt="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560356189p2/47627265.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2036674022?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen">Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2036674022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15532405464282176379"><b>4 Stars</b><br/><i>
  <blockquote>“It does not do to trust people too much.” </blockquote>
</i><br/><br/>Wow! <br/><br/>I was stuck in traffic, so I started this audio book--and an hour later when I finally pulled in my driveway, this was me:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1498760904ra/23160098.gif' width='400' height='200' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Not sure what I expected, but this ended up a fascinating look at mental illness and women’s ‘hysteria.’<br/><br/>Taking place at the end of the 19th century, the story is written as woman's secret journal. She’s married to a physician who has his own treatment plans regarding her depression, or as he refers to it her “nervous </span>
  <span id="freeText15532405464282176379" style="display:none"><b>4 Stars</b><br><i>
  <blockquote>“It does not do to trust people too much.” </blockquote>
</i><br><br>Wow! <br><br>I was stuck in traffic, so I started this audio book--and an hour later when I finally pulled in my driveway, this was me:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1498760904ra/23160098.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Not sure what I expected, but this ended up a fascinating look at mental illness and women’s ‘hysteria.’<br><br>Taking place at the end of the 19th century, the story is written as woman's secret journal. She’s married to a physician who has his own treatment plans regarding her depression, or as he refers to it her “nervous disposition.”<br><br>There was something really terrifying and engrossing about seeing her thoughts gradually shift. As she slowly became more and more obsessed with the wallpaper of her vacation home, she also became less committed to writing her ideas. It was also shockingly sad to see her fears completely dismissed by her husband, and her chosen creative outlet (writing) restricted from her.<br><br>Overall, I see why this is a feminist staple, and loved the writing style. It is quite short, but completely immersive and addicting. I almost wish this had been expanded into a full-length novel, because there's SO MUCH I think would benefit from the elaboration. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15532405464282176379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5689564932596552792" style="display:none">This story is so well written!!! I cannot believe it was written way back in the 1890s.<br /><br />(And people think mental health issues are made-up by the new generation!)<br /><br />Books like this (both fiction and non-fiction) have opened up my eyes regarding mental health. Now I realise the general people and the medics have known the conditions for centuries now. <br /><br />It's the stigma and discrimination like how it is represented in this short story that makes people hush up about these conditions and their consequences.<br /><br />This story is so creepy at times but I was able to relate with the narrator and what she was trying to convey.<br /><br />Most likely that she was suffering from postpartum depression but I can feel the discrimination of the other family members including her 'physician' husband towards her enough to hold her captive.<br /><br />It's the way of human and how our minds work when we might turn into something else in that condition.<br /><br /><br />Sad but stories like this are still happening centuries after!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer190864800515998874">After seeing two recent reviews from two reviewers I respect and getting different perspectives with complete opposing views made me want to pick up this short novella, at 64 pages long it was not much of a stretch to fit it into my day! I'm so glad I picked it up, it really is a peculiar story.   <span id="freeText190864800515998874" style="display:none">After seeing two recent reviews from two reviewers I respect and getting different perspectives with complete opposing views made me want to pick up this short novella, at 64 pages long it was not much of a stretch to fit it into my day! I'm so glad I picked it up, it really is a peculiar story. It captures a real horror of a woman trapped by her nervous disposition as she describes her condition, you really get a real sense of dread at her fixation with the yellow wallpaper in the room where she is recovering from some sort of depressive episode. This was really quite an inventive way to display and portray a woman's descent into madness. I'm glad this was a short story as I felt the power of this wallpaper taking hold! What a wonderful story and that ending!! Interesting! </span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780860682011" href="/book/show/863005.The_Yellow_Wallpaper">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2267158088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9823025946289776792">A story on how some well-meant intentions can have the worst possible consequences. <br/><br/>What I didn't like here was the too sinuous structure of the plot - we see a lot of raving, some fantasies and a bit of reflection of real life. Still, we have no background on anything and this book could have a lot of different twists hidden from us. Due to this it read a bit weird: some gal going crazy about wallpapers supposedly doing sneaky stuff, some guy forbidding his supposedly healthy wife any sort of s</span>
  <span id="freeText9823025946289776792" style="display:none">A story on how some well-meant intentions can have the worst possible consequences. <br /><br />What I didn't like here was the too sinuous structure of the plot - we see a lot of raving, some fantasies and a bit of reflection of real life. Still, we have no background on anything and this book could have a lot of different twists hidden from us. Due to this it read a bit weird: some gal going crazy about wallpapers supposedly doing sneaky stuff, some guy forbidding his supposedly healthy wife any sort of social interaction apart from the domestic kind, any work, any entertainment - why? The question begs: why does this woman have to take 'phosphates or phosphites—whichever it is, and tonics' if her Doctor husband 'assures friends and relatives that there is really nothing the matter with one but temporary nervous depression'?<br /><br />I do appreciate that this structure lets the reader to come to their own conclusions, still I don't like it. Yes, this incolves a lot of things to ponder about roles, pressure and interaction in family and rules and obedience and medical vagaries and psychological maladies treatment... lots of stuff. Still, this is all rather generalistic and this particular short story could have been developed into a proper novel.<br /><br />Q:<br />John is a physician, and perhaps... perhaps that is one reason I do not get well faster. (c)<br />Q:<br />I did write for a while in spite of them; but it does exhaust me a good deal—having to be so sly about it, or else meet with heavy opposition. (c)<br />Q:<br />He is very careful and loving, and hardly lets me stir without special direction. <br />I have a schedule prescription for each hour in the day... It is so hard to talk with John about my case, because he is so wise, and because he loves me so.(c)<br />Q:<br />This paper looks to me as if it knew what a vicious influence it had! (c)<br />Q:<br />I am getting angry enough to do something desperate. To jump out of the window would be admirable exercise, but the bars are too strong even to try.<br />Besides I wouldn’t do it. Of course not. I know well enough that a step like that is improper and might be misconstrued. (c)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9823025946289776792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780146001703" href="/book/show/52302.The_Yellow_Wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6581119-char?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer965195467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9129079027117745556">Medical practices of days gone by can be very scary, judging by today's standards.<br/><br/>Imagine having a bad case of depression. The doctor, and your husband, just tell you to rest. Imagine that...just rest. No books. No writing in diaries or journals. No television. You just rest. Stay right there, and rest. It's no wonder the main character, a woman, becomes obsessed with the wallpaper. What the hell else is there to do? <br/><br/>I would be insane within a week, no lie. If I had books I would be okay, but re</span>
  <span id="freeText9129079027117745556" style="display:none">Medical practices of days gone by can be very scary, judging by today's standards.<br /><br />Imagine having a bad case of depression. The doctor, and your husband, just tell you to rest. Imagine that...just rest. No books. No writing in diaries or journals. No television. You just rest. Stay right there, and rest. It's no wonder the main character, a woman, becomes obsessed with the wallpaper. What the hell else is there to do? <br /><br />I would be insane within a week, no lie. If I had books I would be okay, but reading was too taxing for female folk, according to doctors and the common practices of the day. <br /><br />Can you think of anything else more frightening? Day after day after day, trapped like a prisoner in a room with nothing to occupy your mind? This is a horror story like no other and one of my favorite stories of all time! </span>

    <a title="mark monday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday"><img alt="mark monday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572672245p2/131922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2167038309?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mark monday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mark monday" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday">mark monday</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8217236-the-yellow-wallpaper">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=horror-classic">horror-classic</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167038309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9056512608464411005">There are so many insightful reviews already out there that analyze this absorbing 1892 short story; I fear I have nothing new to add... <br/><br/>Gilman's perfectly sustained masterpiece is a treasure trove and there are many things to contemplate: its proto-feminism and sharp, sharp commentary on how "good men" such as husbands and brothers - and their female allies - strive to keep women passive; its ingenious first person narrative featuring an increasingly unhinged and very unreliable narrator; its d</span>
  <span id="freeText9056512608464411005" style="display:none">There are so many insightful reviews already out there that analyze this absorbing 1892 short story; I fear I have nothing new to add... <br><br>Gilman's perfectly sustained masterpiece is a treasure trove and there are many things to contemplate: its proto-feminism and sharp, sharp commentary on how "good men" such as husbands and brothers - and their female allies - strive to keep women passive; its ingenious first person narrative featuring an increasingly unhinged and very unreliable narrator; its delicate balance between psychological portrait of a descent into madness and eerie supernatural horror set in the confines of a beautiful estate and a disturbingly ugly nursery transformed into our heroine's bedroom - infantilization literalized; its critical examination of so-called "female hysteria"...<br><br><a href="http://s181.photobucket.com/user/markmonday/media/Untitled_zpswrp1xdeh.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509307048i/24308477._SX540_.jpg" alt=" photo Untitled_zpswrp1xdeh.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>...so I'm just going to point out my favorite part of the story: its phenomenal use of the word <i>creeping</i>! can you imagine what a person looks like when they are literally "creeping" about? it's an er creepy image! our heroine describing women who have come from the wallpaper now creeping about on the garden paths was deliciously creepy. our heroine eventually describing herself creeping about her room inspecting the wallpaper's patterns was chilling. and the final scene, when her husband breaks down the door only to find her "creeping" about, promptly faints (I would too), and our heroine has to "creep" over his prone body to continue her examination of the wallpaper... oh my God.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9056512608464411005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
